Gillette Co. v. Commissioner of Revenue


SJC-07330


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


425 Mass. 670; 683 N.E.2d 270; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 192


April 7, 1997, Argued  
July 31, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Appeal from a decision of the Appellate Tax Board. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Decision of Appellate Tax Board affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed a decision of the Appellate Tax Board, which denied plaintiff's applications for abatement of taxes for tax years 1975 through 1983. Plaintiff sought to have defendant commissioner recompute its corporate excise tax using the "unitary method."


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer sought review of defendant commissioner's denial of its applications for abatement of taxes for tax years 1975 through 1983. Plaintiff sought to have defendant recompute its corporate excise tax using the "unitary method" of net income determination and apportionment. The appellate tax board affirmed defendant's decision. On appeal, the court affirmed the denial of the tax abatement, ruling that Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 63, § 42 did not grant statutory authority for the unitary method, therefore it was not a permissible method of computing taxable net income. The court held that plaintiff failed to show by clear and cogent evidence that failure to include foreign subsidiaries' property, payroll, and sales in the apportionment formula was not fair or out of all proportion to income earned in Massachusetts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of plaintiff taxpayer's applications for tax abatements. The court ruled that plaintiff failed to show proof that failure to include foreign subsidiaries' property, payroll, and sales in the apportionment formula was not fair in the apportionment formula.

Commonwealth v. Delaney


SJC-07190


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


425 Mass. 587; 682 N.E.2d 611; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 187


December 4, 1996, Argued  
July 28, 1997, Decided

The cases were tried before Michael T. Stella, Jr., J., and a motion for a new trial was heard by him. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the order of the Lawrence Division of the District Court (Massachusetts), which convicted defendant of five counts of violating an extended protective order that had been issued ex parte, pursuant to Mass. Gen. Law. ch. 209A § 7, and denied his motion for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: The district court extended a protective order against him and found him guilty of five counts of violating that protective order, pursuant to Mass. Gen. Law. ch. 209A § 7. Defendant filed a motion for a new trial and contended, among other things, that because he never was served with the extended order he was charged with violating, he was denied his due process rights under the Fourteenth Amendment. Additionally, defendant alleged that he was prejudiced by the district court's joinder of his charges. The district court denied his motion. The court affirmed the order of the district court and held that personal service of the extended order was not required. The court determined that the ex parte order was delivered and actually received by defendant, which proved that he had actual knowledge of the extended order. The court concluded that the trial court had properly joined the actions against defendant because the offenses all arose from parts of a single scheme or plan.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the district court.

 

36 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


League of Women Voters v. Secretary of the Commonwealth


SJC-07394


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


425 Mass. 424; 681 N.E.2d 842; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 165


May 6, 1997, Argued  
July 11, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on December 8, 1995. The case was reported by Abrams, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment entered in county court declaring St. 1994, c. 230, unconstitutional insofar as it seeks to limit access to primary and general election ballots to certain State officers identified in chapter 230 and seeks to eliminate payment of compensation and benefits to certain of those State officers if they should be reelected in an election in which their names are not printed on official ballot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff voters' organization initially challenged the constitutionality of a proposed law, which was approved as an initiative petition in the Supreme Judicial Court for the County of Suffolk (Massachusetts). The voters' organization contended that the people could not legislate qualifications for public officers in addition to those qualifications that appeared in the constitution.


OVERVIEW: The voters' group questioned the constitutionality of a proposed law, arising from an initiative, that would have prevented an incumbent's name from being printed on a state ballot as a candidate after the incumbent had served a certain number of consecutive terms, and that would have prevented those persons so elected from collecting a salary. The court held that the proposed law was unconstitutional insofar as it sought to limit access to election ballots and sought to eliminate the compensation and benefits for incumbents reelected in an election in which their names were not printed on the ballot. The court found that the constitution established the qualifications of candidates and that it did not authorize the people to change the qualifications by initiative. Limiting the right of incumbent candidates to have their name on the ballots was tantamount to evading the constitution's requirements. Although Mass. Const. Declaration of Rights arts. 8, 9 granted the right of the people to enact laws, the people failed to grant themselves the authority by the initiative process to prescribe qualifications for office beyond those stated in the constitution.


OUTCOME: The court held that the proposed law was unconstitutional insofar as it sought to limit access to primary and general election ballots to certain state officers and sought to eliminate the payment of compensation and benefits to certain of those state officers if they were reelected in an election in which their names were not printed on the official ballot.

Daddario v. Cape Cod Comm'n


SJC-07329


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


425 Mass. 411; 681 N.E.2d 833; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 162


March 4, 1997, Argued  
July 11, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended. Certiorari Denied December 15, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 7641.
Writ of certiorari denied Daddario v. Cape Cod Comm'n, 522 U.S. 1036, 118 S. Ct. 644, 139 L. Ed. 2d 621, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 7641 (1997)
Findings of fact/conclusions of law at, On remand at Daddario v. Cape Cod Comm'n, 1999 Mass. LCR LEXIS 10 (1999)
Count dismissed at, On remand at Daddario v. Cape Cod Comm'n, 2000 Mass. LCR LEXIS 30 (2000)


PRIOR HISTORY: Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Land Court Department on March 1, 1995. The case was heard by Robert V. Cauchon, J. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.
Daddario v. Cape Cod Comm'n, 1996 Mass. LCR LEXIS 53 (1996)



DISPOSITION: Judgment vacated and matter remanded to Land Court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff developer filed suit against defendant Cape Cod Commission (commission), seeking judicial review and declaratory relief from the commission's denial of a development permit. The Land Court (Massachusetts) determined that the denial of the permit constituted a taking under both the federal and the Massachusetts constitutions and, under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 716 (1989), ordered the commission to approve the development.


OVERVIEW: The developer requested a permit to remove sand and gravel from 35 acres that were zoned as agricultural. The plan included reclamation as required by the local zoning by-laws. The municipal authority referred the request to the commission, who denied the permit. The developer filed suit under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 716, § 17(b) (1989). The land court determined that even though the land might have had substantial value for other development, the denial of the permit was a "taking." On appeal, the court held that the issue was not yet ripe for adjudication because there had not yet been a final determination on the nature and extent of the development that the commission would permit. The court noted that the commission had proposed an alternate plan to permit sand and gravel extraction on 25 acres, and held that this proposal demonstrated that the commission was willing to consider less extensive mining proposals. The court noted that the property had substantial value for alternative uses permitted under the applicable zoning regulations and held that the developer had not shown that there was a taking.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the land court's judgment and remanded for further proceedings.

Commonwealth v. Fryar


SJC-07116


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


425 Mass. 237; 680 N.E.2d 901; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 132


February 4, 1997, Argued  
June 13, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied December 15, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 7588.


PRIOR HISTORY: Hampden. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on May 10, 1989. Following review by this court, 414 Mass. 732 (1993), the cases were tried before Richard F. Connon, J. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court Department (Massachusetts), which convicted him of murder in the second-degree and two counts of assault and battery by means of a dangerous weapon. Defendant had been involved in a brawl outside a bar, which culminated in the stabbing death of a student.


OVERVIEW: A street fight broke out between a group of African-American youths and a group of white college students outside a bar. The brawl culminated in the stabbing death of a student. No one saw the stabbing. However, witnesses observed defendant swinging a stick and sparring with the victim. Defendant was taken into custody, confessed to stabbing the victim, and was indicted by a grand jury. Defendant was convicted of murder in the second-degree and two counts of assault and battery by means of a dangerous weapon. The court affirmed defendant's convictions because: (1) the process for selecting the grand and petit jury venire did not violate the Constitution; (2) the jury instructions concerning malice, the state's burden of proof, and the trial judge's refusal to instruct on involuntary manslaughter were not improper; and (3) the trial court's evidentiary rulings were proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for murder in the second-degree and two counts of assault and battery by means of a dangerous weapon.

Doherty v. Retirement Bd.


SJC-07325


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


425 Mass. 130; 680 N.E.2d 45; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 123


February 6, 1997, Submitted  
June 5, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Middlesex. Civil action commenced in the Somerville Division of the District Court Department on June 24, 1993. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on March 10, 1994. A motion to dismiss was heard by Isaac Borenstein, J., and, after an amendment of the pleadings was ordered by him, the case was heard by Judith A. Cowin, J., on a motion for summary judgment. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of Superior Court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff police officer appealed the decision of the Superior Court Department (Massachusetts), which upheld the decision of the district court. The district court upheld the decision of defendant retirement board, which found the officer guilty of misappropriating city funds, pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 32, §15(1). The board held that the officer lost his entitlement to recover retirement deductions accumulated during years of service.


OVERVIEW: The officer's employment was terminated after he was convicted of two felonies, one of which arose from a scheme in which the officer stole and sold advance copies of the police entrance and promotion examinations and answers. The officer had supplied his son with a copy of the entrance exam that the son then used to obtain employment as a police officer. The court found no grounds for reversal. The subject statute was clearly restitutionary, and the officer was not deprived of property that exceeded his misappropriation. Further, the statute was not so punitive as to overcome its restitutionary purpose. The officer was accorded all of the process to which he was due under the statute because he was granted a hearing at which he was permitted to present evidence, cross-examine, and seek judicial review. The board's decision was supported by substantial evidence. Issues of credibility as to a witness against the officer were for the board to determine. The officer was properly held responsible for misappropriation because he caused payments to be made to his son, in the form of paychecks fraudulently obtained by the son through the use of the stolen entrance exam and answers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court, which upheld the denial of the officer's retirement deductions.

Barnes v. Metropolitan Hous. Assistance Program


SJC-07367


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


425 Mass. 79; 679 N.E.2d 545; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 115


March 6, 1997, Argued  
May 22, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on April 1, 1991. On transfer to the Boston Division of the Housing Court Department, a motion for summary judgment was heard by E. George Daher, J., and the case was reported by him to the Appeals Court. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case.



DISPOSITION: Order of Housing Court denying summary judgment with respect to Count I vacated. In respect to Count II, case is remanded to that court for further proceedings in accordance with opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tenants filed an action in contract and in tort against defendant state housing agency alleging that its breach of the obligation to inspect led to lead poisoning of the minor tenant. The Boston Division of the Housing Court Department (Massachusetts) denied the state housing agency's motion for summary judgment, and the parties filed a motion requesting appellate review of certain underlying questions.


OVERVIEW: The tenants moved into a rental unit subsidized by the state housing agency. The minor tenant later developed symptoms of lead poisoning, and it was determined that she had ingested paint chips containing lead. The tenants claimed that the state housing agency breached a duty to inspect the premises arising out of the contract between the owner of the units and the state housing agency. The tenants claimed that they were intended third party beneficiaries of the contract. They also claimed that the state housing agency was liable in tort under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 258, § 10(j)(1). The court held that the state housing agency was entitled to judgment because (1) the clause excluding liability to third parties demonstrated that the tenants were not intended beneficiaries of the contract; and (2) there was no basis for a tort claim because the tenants failed to show that the state housing agency had made an explicit and specific assurance of safety to the tenants.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the denial of the motion for summary judgment as to the breach of contract claim, but dismissed the tort claim without prejudice and remanded it for further proceedings.

 

42 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Gauthier


SJC-07369


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


425 Mass. 37; 679 N.E.2d 211; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 111


March 5, 1997, Argued  
May 16, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Essex. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on May 19, 1993. A pretrial motion to suppress evidence was heard by Elizabeth B. Donovan, J., and the cases were tried before Thomas E. Connolly, J. After review by the Appeals Court, the Supreme Judicial Court granted leave to obtain further appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Judgments reversed and verdicts set aside. Order entered allowing defendant's motion to suppress.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Commonwealth challenged a ruling of the Appeals Court (Massachusetts), which reversed defendant's convictions for narcotics trafficking and possession, and illegal possession of a firearm. The lower court held that an anticipatory search warrant could not support a search under U.S. Const. amend. IV unless the triggering event appeared on the face of, or attached to, the warrant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was suspected of supplying drug dealers with marijuana. The police obtained an anticipatory search warrant that complied with Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 276, § 2A, by stating a clear triggering event for the search, that being the departure from the premises of a certain drug dealer if carrying marijuana. The event was not set forth in the warrant or attached to the warrant. The drug dealer left the premises but was apprehended far away; marijuana was found but its source was not known. Defendant's home was searched regardless. The court found that this was improper because the triggering event, as described, had not occurred. Consequently, defendant's motion to suppress should have been allowed. The court disagreed with the lower appellate court's reasoning, explicitly holding that the search warrant did not need to contain the description of the triggering event. The court stated that it was sufficient if it was described in the affidavit making application for the warrant. The court held that neither U.S. Const. amend. IV nor Mass. Const. art. 14 required a description of the triggering event in the warrant itself or attached to the warrant.


OUTCOME: The court, using a different rationale, affirmed the lower appellate judgment, which reversed defendant's convictions for narcotics trafficking and possession, and illegal possession of a firearm. The court ordered the verdicts set aside and ordered that defendant's motion to suppress be granted.

Benefit v. City of Cambridge


SJC-07341


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


424 Mass. 918; 679 N.E.2d 184; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 108


April 8, 1997, Argued  
May 14, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Middlesex. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on July 9, 1992. The case was heard by Catherine A. White, J., on motions for summary judgment. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent complainant filed an action seeking a declaration that petitioner city's panhandling law, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 272, § 66, was unconstitutional. The Superior Court (Massachusetts) ordered that the statute be declared unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV, and enjoined the city from enforcing it. The court granted the city's application for direct appellate review.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that Mass. Gen. Stat. ch. 272, § 66 violated the First Amendment because it banned constitutionally protected speech in traditional public forums. In so holding, the court first determined that, despite the fact that criminal charges against complainant were disposed of during the pendency of the civil action, a real dispute continued to exist, and the complainant had a sufficient personal interest in the rights and relief at stake to meet standing requirements to seek declaratory relief. The court then considered the merits, concluding that (1) the peaceful begging engaged in by the complainant was similar to soliciting funds for charities and therefore involved communicative activity protected by the First Amendment; (2) the criminal sanction, imposed on such activity by ch. 272, § 66, was content-and viewpoint-based and banned the activity in traditional public forums; and (3) as a result, the statute was subject to strict scrutiny, a test which the statute failed as there was no compelling state interest warranting punishment for panhandling.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's order granting a preliminary injunction entered pursuant to its declaration that the panhandling statute violated the First Amendment.

Adoption of Quentin


SJC-07285


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


424 Mass. 882; 678 N.E.2d 1325; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 104


December 3, 1996, Argued  
May 13, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Petitions filed in the Boston Division of the Juvenile Court Department, three on August 19, 1992, and three on September 1, 1993, respectively. The cases were heard by Stephen M. Limon, J. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.



DISPOSITION: Order granting petition to dispense with consent to adoption of three children affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents challenged an order from the Probate and Family Court (Massachusetts) awarding custody of their three children to petitioner Department of Social Services' (department) and granting the department's petition to dispense with parental consent to adoption. Among other things, the parents argued that the evidentiary standard to demonstrate their current parental unfitness was not met.


OVERVIEW: In 1983 the father joined the Orlo Templi Orientis and studied the so-called "Satanic Bible." He was convicted of grave robbing. The first of the parent's three children were born in 1987. The family separated for a time. They reunited in Cambridge and begged in Harvard Square. The department conducted an investigation finding that the mother was of borderline intelligence and that the father used her public assistance money to buy drugs. The oldest child was diagnosed as suffering from post traumatic distress syndrome. She told the investigators that her father was a "witch" and took pictures of her with her clothes off. She also described sexual touching. One of the sons also described sexual abuse and exhibited inappropriate, aggressive sexual behavior. The children were found to be filthy. They did not understand even basic personal hygiene. The court ruled that the children's out-of-court statements concerning the sexual abuse were not necessary to support a conclusion of parental unfitness. The court, therefore, so did not rule on the constitutional challenge the parents made to admission of the statements, although it analyzed the issue for the benefit of future cases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting the petition to dispense with consent to adoption of the parents' three children.

 

45 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Sbordone


SJC-07143


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


424 Mass. 802; 678 N.E.2d 1184; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 93; 68 A.L.R.5th 801


December 5, 1996, Argued  
May 1, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on October 18, 1993. A pretrial motion to suppress evidence was heard by Diane M. Kottmyer, J. An application for an interlocutory appeal was allowed by Wilkins, J., in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk, and the appeal was reported by him.



DISPOSITION: Order of suppression vacated and case remanded to Superior Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth review of an order of the Superior Court Department (Massachusetts), which granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence seized against him.


OVERVIEW: After evidence that was seized from his office formed the basis of his criminal indictments, defendant filed a motion to suppress the evidence. Defendant alleged that because the search warrant had been executed with the active participation of a civilian it was executed in violation of Mass. Const. Declaration of Rights art. 14 and Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 276, § 2. The superior court suppressed the evidence; however, on appeal, the court vacated and remanded the superior court's order. The court held that the superior court judge had correctly determined that although civilian participation in the execution of search warrants was permissible in certain circumstances, the civilian had exceeded the scope of his permissive participation in the search against defendant. However, the court held that the evidence was not automatically excludable because the evidence would still have been discovered, so it was admissible under the inevitable discovery doctrine. Finally, the court held that the civilian participation was not so severe as to require exclusion because the civilian was a commonwealth investigator who had actively investigated defendant prior to the search.


OUTCOME: The court vacated and remanded an order of the superior court, which granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence seized against him.

Tarin v. Commissioner of the Div. of Medical Assistance


SJC-07247


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


424 Mass. 743; 678 N.E.2d 146; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 88


January 6, 1997, Argued  
April 17, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Middlesex. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on December 21, 1993. The case was heard by Judith A. Cowin, J. the Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Medicaid recipient sought review of a decision of appellee Division of Medical Assistance (division) that his court-ordered child support payments were income available to him for the purpose of calculating his contribution to the cost of his nursing home care. The recipient challenged when the superior court affirmed the division's decision and the court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court (Massachusetts).


OVERVIEW: The recipient argued that the Medicaid Act, 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 1396 et seq., and its regulations unconstitutionally discriminated against divorced recipients and their children, and that his court-ordered child support payments were not "available" for purposes of calculating his patient-paid amount. The court held that because no contribution was required from the recipient's former spouse, and the former spouse's assets were not included when determining the recipient's eligibility for benefits, it was rational for the division to disallow a deduction for the family's maintenance needs. The disallowance was not an invasion of the recipient's liberty interests. The state was not constitutionally obligated to subsidize his support payments. That the recipient no longer had the financial resources to support his children as he once agreed did not mean that any coercive constitutional burdens were placed on him. The division's decision to include the child support payments in the recipient's available income was not arbitrary, capricious, or contrary to law. The regulations were consistent with the statutory scheme and with the reasonable exercise of authority by the division.


OUTCOME: The finding that child support payments were available income for the purpose of Medicaid contributions was affirmed.

 

47 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Bonds


SJC-07196


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


424 Mass. 698; 677 N.E.2d 1131; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 85


March 5, 1997, Argued  
April 9, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Hampden. Indictment found and returned in the Superior Court on September 14, 1972. A motion for a new trial, filed on September 14, 1995, was heard by John F. Moriarty, J. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Trial judge's order allowing defendant's motion for new trial affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Several decades after defendant was convicted for murder in the second degree, the Superior Court (Massachusetts) granted defendant's motion for a new trial. The state appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of murder in the second degree and sentenced to life in prison. A claim of appeal was entered, but an order of dismissal was later docketed. Defendant eventually was released on parole but was reincarcerated after his parole was revoked. A single justice of the appellate court reinstated defendant's appellate right and granted defendant leave to file a motion for a new trial in the trial court. The trial court granted defendant's motion, holding that the jury instructions on reasonable doubt were constitutionally deficient. The instructions equated proof beyond a reasonable doubt with moral certainty and described moral certainty as the level of certainty required in making decisions in matters of the highest importance concerning one's own personal affairs. The trial court then explained the meaning of moral certainty through the use of illustrations involving the decisions to marry, buy a new house, or leave a long held job. The appellate court agreed that the instructions could not withstand constitutional scrutiny. Hence, the court affirmed the trial court's order allowing defendant's motion for a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order allowing defendant's motion for a new trial.

 

48 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Amirault


SJC-07077, SJC-07153


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


424 Mass. 618; 677 N.E.2d 652; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 74


October 9, 1996, Argued  
March 24, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended April 30, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Middlesex. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on January 21, 1985, and September 19, 1985. A motion for a new trial, filed on March 30, 1995, was heard by Elizabeth J. Dolan, J. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department, six on January 21, 1985, and six on September 19, 1985. A motion for a new trial, filed on April 11, 1995, was heard by Robert A. Barton, J. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth sought direct appellate review of decisions of the Superior Court Department (Massachusetts), which granted new trials to two defendants and denied a new trial to a third in cases arising out of the same allegations of child abuse. All the defendants had argued that special seating arrangements deprived them of their right to meet witnesses against them face to face as guaranteed by Mass. Const. Declaration of Rights art. 12.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were found guilty of several counts of rape of a child and indecent assault and battery on a child in two severed trials arising from the same allegations of abuse. The female defendants' request for a new trial, on the grounds that special seating arrangements for the child witnesses deprived them of their right to meet witnesses against them face to face as guaranteed by Mass. Const. Declaration of Rights art. 12, was granted; the male defendant's similar request was denied. The court reversed the grant of a new trial to the female defendants and affirmed the denial of a new trial to the male defendant. The court held that, although defendants' right to have the witnesses face them was violated by the seating arrangement, in which witnesses were seated facing the jury, defendants did not object even when the judge suggested he would consider confrontation clause objections. Further, defendants did not meet their burden of showing there was a substantial risk of a miscarriage of justice in the waiver because all of the witnesses testified under oath in the physical presence of the defendants and there were several actual face-to-face encounters throughout the trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of a new trial to two defendants and affirmed the denial of a new trial to the third because, although the special seating arrangements deprived them of their right to meet witnesses against them face to face, defendants waived that right by failing to object to the arrangements despite reasonable opportunity to do so. Further, the waiver of that right did not raise a substantial risk of a miscarriage of justice.

Planned Parenthood League of Mass. Inc. v. Attorney General


SJC-07121


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


424 Mass. 586; 677 N.E.2d 101; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 72


November 5, 1996, Argued  
March 18, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on September 25, 1995. The case was reported by Greaney, J.



DISPOSITION: Order shall be entered in county court denying motion to intervene of Massachusetts Citizens for Life. Judgment shall be entered in county court declaring parental consent and judicial approval provisions of G. L. c. 112, § 12S, do not violate due process and equal protection provisions of Constitution of Commonwealth, except requirement that more than one parent give consent to performance of unmarried minor's abortion violates due process provisions of Constitution of Commonwealth and § 12S shall be enforced as if it stated requirement only of one-parent consent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, abortion services providers, a physician, and an unmarried pregnant minor, and defendant attorney general sought summary judgment in an injunction action seeking to permanently enjoin the attorney general from enforcing the two-parent parental consent or judicial approval requirement of Mass. Gen. Laws. ch. 112, § 12S. A group opposed to abortion sought to intervene as a defendant.


OVERVIEW: The court held that § 12S's parental consent-judicial bypass provision did not, on its face, deny a pregnant minor due process of law. The claim that a pregnant unmarried minor was denied equal protection of the laws failed because the classification made by § 12S had a rational basis. However, the requirement that, with certain exceptions, the minor had to obtain the consent of both parents to her having an abortion, or else had to seek judicial approval, lacked sufficient justification to overcome the burden that the two-parent consent requirement placed on the minor's constitutional right to choose. To require that the minor follow a judicial bypass process when the purpose of parental consent was fulfilled by the consent of one parent would burden the minor's constitutional rights without adequate justification. The court held that the attorney general conscientiously and professionally represented the interests of the group opposed to abortion, and intervention, as opposed to status as a friend of the court filing a brief, was not needed for the group to make its point that the judicial bypass made unimportant any defect in the two-parent consent requirement.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that the statute was to be enforced as if it stated a requirement only of one-parent consent. The court denied the motion to intervene.

 

50 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Planned Parenthood League v. Bell


SJC-07004


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


424 Mass. 573; 677 N.E.2d 204; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 71


November 5, 1996, Argued  
March 18, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied October 6, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4744.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Middlesex. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on October 21, 1994. The case was heard by Margaret R. Hinkle, J., and a motion for reconsideration was heard by her. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff clinic brought an action that sought injunctive relief against defendant protester based on common law public nuisance grounds. The protester challenged the imposition of a preliminary injunction prohibiting her from entering within fifty feet of the clinic and the Appeals Court (Massachusetts) denied the protester's petition for temporary relief pending appeal. The court transferred the appeal on its own motion.


OVERVIEW: The trespasser alleged that the clinic had no standing to obtain the injunction, that the injunction infringed on her constitutional rights, and that the injunction was overbroad. The clinic argued that it had representational standing to argue the rights of its non-party patients. The court held that because access to abortion was at issue, the clinic had standing to bring the action against the protester. The protester's activity was outside of the realm of constitutionally protected speech. U.S. Const. amend. I did not shelter the trespasser's practice of hollering into the clinic, blocking or obstructing patients, or her persistent violation of an existing court order against such conduct. The governmental interest in ensuring public safety, protecting a woman's freedom to seek lawful medical and counseling services, protecting the physical and psychological well-being of patients, and maintaining the integrity of court orders were significant enough to justify an appropriately tailored injunction to secure unimpeded physical access to the clinics. The distance restriction imposed burdened no more speech than necessary to serve the significant public interests at stake.


OUTCOME: The injunction against the protester was affirmed.

 

51 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Richardson v. Dailey


SJC-07131


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


424 Mass. 258; 675 N.E.2d 787; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 45


November 4, 1996, Argued  
February 13, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on September 6, 1991. The case was heard by Thayer Fremont-Smith, J., on motions for summary judgment. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the wife and executor of the estate of a decedent who committed suicide while in a holding cell, appealed a decision of the Superior Court (Massachusetts), which granted summary judgment to defendants, the city and commonwealth, in a claim alleging wrongful death, negligence, and violation of the decedent's rights under 42 U.S.C.S. 1983.


OVERVIEW: The wife brought an action against the city alleging negligence, wrongful death, civil rights violation, and related claims for failure to take action to protect the decedent while in custody. The lower court entered summary judgment for the city, and the court transferred the case on its own motion on appeal. The court found no evidence the city knew the decedent was suicidal even though he had complained of drug withdrawal, and therefore summary judgment was proper on the negligence claim. The court also found that the wife failed to strictly comply with Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 258, § 4 because her written claim described the wrongful acts of the court employees but not the city, and there was no proper presentment. Therefore summary judgment in favor of the city was proper. Further, the wife failed to prove deliberate indifference to support a claim under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 against the commonwealth because the decedent did not appear suicidal. Nor could a § 1983 claim be maintained against the city for failure to maintain the holding cell because there was no regulatory violation and no evidence of deliberate indifference by the city.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed summary judgment in favor of the city and commonwealth in the wife's lawsuit alleging wrongful death, negligence, and violation of the civil rights of the decedent.

In re Corliss


SJC-07108


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


424 Mass. 1005; 675 N.E.2d 398; 1997 Mass. LEXIS 34



February 7, 1997, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent board of bar examiners determined that petitioner attorney was ineligible to take the limited written bar examination required for admission on motion to the bar under Mass. Sup. Judicial Ct. R. 3:01, § 6.1. The attorney appealed the order, and a single justice of the court dismissed his appeal. The attorney appealed from the order of dismissal.


OVERVIEW: The attorney sought a waiver from the provisions of Mass. Sup. Judicial Ct. R. 3:01, § 6.1.4, which required graduation from a law school approved by the American Bar Association (ABA). The attorney graduated from a law school in California that was not ABA approved. After passing the California bar examination, the attorney practiced law in California for 12 years. The court's rules committee declined to grant a waiver to the attorney. The attorney alleged that he was denied equal protection because, unlike him, graduates of foreign law schools were eligible for admission even though their schools were not ABA approved. The court rejected the attorney's argument, and affirmed the single justice's dismissal. The court held that it was reasonable for the court to provide an independent evaluation for graduates of foreign law schools because the ABA did not evaluate foreign law schools. The court also rejected the attorney's argument that graduates of Massachusetts law schools not approved by the ABA were eligible to take the Massachusetts bar examination. The court noted that those individuals were only eligible to take the general bar examination, not the limited examination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the single justice's dismissal of the attorney's appeal of the board's determination that the attorney was ineligible to take the limited written bar examination.

O'Brien's Case


SJC-07058


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


424 Mass. 16; 673 N.E.2d 567; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 328


October 8, 1996, Argued  
December 12, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Appeal from a decision of the Industrial Accident Reviewing Board. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.



DISPOSITION: Appeal dismissed, and stay should be vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a hearing under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 152, § 11A(2), the claimant's worker's compensation benefits were discontinued. The Industrial Accident Reviewing Board (Massachusetts) remanded but opined that § 11A was unconstitutional on its face and stayed the remand for the claimant to appeal the constitutional question. A single justice of the appeals court reported the case to a panel of the court, which took the case on its own motion.


OVERVIEW: Claimant argued that § 11A was unconstitutional on its face in that it did not allow either party to present as of right its own medical testimony in addition to that of the impartial medical examiner. The court found as an initial matter that resolution of the question was sufficiently important that it was prudent for the court to answer it even though aspects of justiciability were missing. Applying the Mathews due process analysis, the court weighed two matters: the governmental interest in giving the impartial report "prima facie" status and the effect of that status in the scheme in which contestants' rights were adjudicated. The court found that the government's interest in efficiency benefited workers, employers, and insurers and did not come at too high a price. The court reasoned that § 11A(2) provided explicitly that the administrative judge could authorize the submission of additional medical testimony when necessary. There was no rule of due process that gave a party an unrestricted right to present any testimony he chose. Claimant had sufficient opportunity to develop medical testimony she considered favorable to her claim.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the statute was not unconstitutional on its face, dismissed the appeal, and noted that the stay should be vacated.

Opinion of the Justices to the House of Representatives


SJC-07309


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


423 Mass. 1244; 673 N.E.2d 36; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 317



November 26, 1996, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] First question answered in the negative. Justices ask to be excused from answering second question.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The House of Representatives of the Commonwealth of Massachusetts adopted an order transmitting two questions to the court with the request that the court provide answers to the questions, which concerned a proposed amendment to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 151B, § 4(1A) (1994). The court had previously held that § 4(1A) violated the establishment clause of the First Amendment.


OVERVIEW: The prior § 4(1A) had protected an employee against being required to work on a day of religious observance if the employee's sincerely held religious belief was shared by others belonging to an organized church or sect, but not if such a belief was not so similarly shared. The questions asked whether the amended § 4(1A), which defined the term "creed or religion" to encompass sincerely held beliefs regardless of whether they were approved by an established church, violated the establishment clause and the state constitution, and whether its retroactive applicability violated due process. The court answered the first question in the negative, stating that the amendment removed the constitutional violations previously identified, and that by permitting an employer to show that accommodation to an employee's religious observance would cause undue hardship it took account of the employer's interests. The court set forth relevant considerations but determined that it should not definitively answer the second question, because the answer would depend on the facts of each case and the court had been unwilling to provide opinions on issues that could be raised in pending litigation.


OUTCOME: The court answered the first question, on whether the amended statute would violate the establishment clause and the corresponding state constitutional provision, "No," respectfully asked to be excused from further answering the second question, and submitted its answer and opinion.

Mendonza v. Commonwealth


SJC-06957, SJC-07142


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


423 Mass. 771; 673 N.E.2d 22; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 310


September 10, 1996, Argued  
November 14, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on July 5, 1995. The case was heard by Greaney, J. Complaint received and sworn to in the Somerville Division of the District Court department on July 3, 1995. A question of law was reported to the Appeals Court by Mark S. Coven, J. The Supreme Judicial Court granted a request for direct review.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of single justice as to Mendonza's claim affirmed. As to Callender, the answer to the question reported to us is that properly construed to require good cause § 58A (4) is not unconstitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of a single justice of the court, which denied defendant relief after a detention order was issued under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 276, § 58A (1994) for dangerousness. The court also granted direct appellate review to a separate defendant on the constitutionality of § 58A at the request of the Appeals Court of Massachusetts. Because both appeals addressed the constitutionality of § 58A, the court consolidated the appeals.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with numerous violent crimes. After defendant was indicted, the commonwealth sought a detention order for dangerousness under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 276, § 58A (1994), which was granted after a hearing. Defendant appealed to a single justice of the court, challenging the constitutionality of § 58A. The justice denied relief, and defendant appealed. A separate defendant sought direct appellate review of the constitutionality of a three-day continuance prior to a hearing for dangerousness under § 58A. The court consolidated the appeals. The court held that § 58A did not violate defendant's due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Mass. Const. art. I, X, XII, and XXVI, because it required probable cause that defendant committed a serious crime in addition to clear and convincing evidence of his future dangerousness. Section 58A did not contradict the provisions of the Bail Reform Act of 1984. A three-day continuance under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 276, § 58A(4), prior to the detention hearing was not unconstitutional because the commonwealth was required to show good cause for it. The continuance properly allowed police to gather evidence for the hearing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying relief to defendant and ruled against a separate defendant on the issue on direct review. A state law allowing the judge to detain defendant prior to trial based on dangerousness was not unconstitutional because it required probable cause that a serious crime was committed. A three-day continuance prior to the detention hearing was not unconstitutional because the commonwealth had to show good cause.

Tax Equity Alliance v. Commissioner of Revenue


SJC-07185


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


423 Mass. 708; 672 N.E.2d 504; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 304


September 9, 1996, Argued  
November 8, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on February 9, 1995. The case was reported by Lynch, J.



DISPOSITION: Order denying commissioner's motion to dismiss vacated. Order is to enter in county court allowing motion, followed by judgment dismissing complaint on ground that plaintiffs lack standing to sue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Commissioner of Revenue appealed an order of the Supreme Judicial Court for the County of Suffolk (Massachusetts), which denied a motion to dismiss the complaint brought by plaintiffs, tax activism group and taxpayers. The tax activism group and the taxpayers challenged the constitutionality of provisions contained in 1994 Mass. Acts ch. 195.


OVERVIEW: The tax activism group and the taxpayers filed the action to challenge the constitutionality of certain provisions in ch. 195 that had amended Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 62, governing taxation of capital gains. The taxpayers contended that the provisions violated Mass. Const. art. XLIV, which required a single taxation rate on income derived from a single class of property, because capital assets would have been taxed at more than one rate under 1994 Mass. Acts ch. 195. The taxpayers sought declaratory relief enjoining the Commissioner of Revenue from spending money to enforce ch. 195, and requiring the Commissioner of Revenue administer laws as they stood before ch. 195. The taxpayers claimed they had standing under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 29, § 63 (1994) and under the "public right doctrine." The court vacated the lower court's order denying dismissal and ordered dismissal. The court found that the taxpayers lacked standing because 1994 Mass. Acts ch. 195 itself did not call for the disputed expenditures. The court also found that the taxpayers lacked standing because the public right doctrine did not apply to substantive constitutional challenges and they had not suffered harm.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order denying dismissal and directed that the complaint be dismissed for lack of standing.

Pielech v. Massasoit Greyhound


SJC-06785


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


423 Mass. 534; 668 N.E.2d 1298; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 212; 71 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1145; 68 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P44,187


November 7, 1995, Argued  
August 20, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied March 17, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 1878.
Writ of certiorari denied Pielech v. Massasoit Greyhound, 520 U.S. 1131, 137 L. Ed. 2d 356, 117 S. Ct. 1280, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 1878 (1997)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Bristol. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on June 15, 1993. The case was heard by John J. O'Brien, J., on motions for summary judgment. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.
Pielech v. Massasoit Greyhound, 1994 Mass. Super. LEXIS 653 (Mass. Super. Ct., 1994)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff at-will employees appealed a ruling of the Bristol County Superior Court (Massachusetts), which granted summary judgment to defendant employer on the employees' suit for damages from being forced to work on Christmas Day in spite of Mass. Gen. Laws. ch. 151B, § 4(1A) (1994) and the Massachusetts Equal Rights Act (ERA), Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 93, § 102 (1994).


OVERVIEW: At-will employees who worked as parimutuel clerks at the greyhound race track were told that they would work on Christmas Day. The employees refused to work and were discharged. They brought suit, claiming that their civil and religious rights were violated. The court on appeal found that the employer was entitled to summary judgment, but for the reason that the statute relied upon, Mass. Gen. However, the court declined to revise the statute to effect a different outcome. The court also held that the statute promoted excessive governmental entanglement with religion, which was also a violation of the First Amendment. The court found that the ERA contention was insufficiently briefed to warrant appellate review.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment granted to the employer because the statute relied upon by the employees violated the First Amendment.

Opinion of the Justices to the Senate


SJC-07224


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


423 Mass. 1201; 668 N.E.2d 738; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 168


July 18, 1996, Submitted  
July 18, 1996, Issued


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Massachusetts Senate sought an advisory opinion, pursuant to Mass. Const. Part II, ch. 3, art. 2, as amended by art. 85 of the Amendments, to determine whether proposed sex offender registration legislation, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 6 § 174B, violated the United States Constitution or state constitution.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the proposed sex offender registration law was constitutional because the new law was not a violation of: (1) the ex post facto provision because it was not designed to punish, but to regulate; (2) procedural due process because the procedures met the constitutional requirements of a civil, remedial standard; (3) equal protection because the law was not overinclusive; (4) the right to privacy because the state's regulatory interest in public safety outweighed any stigma attached to being a known, convicted sex offender; (5) the Eighth Amendment because the statute also applied to out-of-state convicted sex offenders, whom Massachusetts had no authority to punish; (6) the Double Jeopardy Clause because the law was a civil sanction imposed for the same actions on which a criminal penalty had previously been imposed and was proportionate to the law's remedial aims.


OUTCOME: The court found that the proposed Massachusetts sex offender registration legislation did not violate the constitution. The court advised that its advisory opinion was limited to the state senate's specific request and was not definitive in its answer.

 

60 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Greenfield Country Estates Tenants Ass'n v. Deep


SJC-07047


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


423 Mass. 81; 666 N.E.2d 988; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 148


January 9, 1996, Argued  
June 26, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Franklin. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on May 11, 1994. The case was heard by Francis X. Spina, J., on a motion for partial summary judgment. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Award of summary judgment in favor of the association affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant buyer appealed a partial summary judgment awarded by the Superior Court (Massachusetts) to plaintiff tenants' association in its claim to exercise its right of first refusal for purchase of a manufactured housing community under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 140, § 32R.


OVERVIEW: On learning of the sale of the manufactured housing community where its members lived, plaintiff tenants' association requested information regarding the terms of the sale from defendant buyer. Plaintiff planned to tender an offer to purchase the property on substantially similar terms in an effort to exercise its statutory right of first refusal under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 140, § 32R. Defendant refused to provide the information and plaintiff filed suit. The lower court granted plaintiff's motion for partial summary judgment and awarded specific performance. On appeal, the court affirmed. The statute did not create an unconstitutional taking. It served the legitimate goals of avoiding discontinuances of manufactured housing communities and ensuring that tenants were not left at the peril of their landlords due to a practical inability to relocate a manufactured housing unit. It did not deprive an owner of economically viable use of the land. The owner was not required to sell to tenants on terms less favorable than could be received from a third party. The award of specific performance was wholly consistent with both the common law and the mandates of Mass. Gen. Laws. ch. 93A.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the partial summary judgment in favor of plaintiff tenants' association in its claim against defendant buyer to exercise its statutory right of first refusal for purchase of a manufactured housing community. The statute did not effect a taking because it served a legitimate state goal of avoiding the disappearance of manufactured housing communities and it did not deprive an owner of economically viable use of the land.

Anderson v. Attorney Gen.


SJC-07214


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


422 Mass. 809; 666 N.E.2d 118; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 135


May 20, 1994, Argued  
June 3, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on May 8, 1996. The case was reported by Lynch, J.



DISPOSITION: Remanded to county court for entry of judgment declaring Attorney General's denial of proposed amendments to initiative was within reasonable exercise of duties under art. 48.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, two individuals and the organizations they represented (sponsors), brought this action against the attorney general that sought relief in the nature of a mandamus and declaratory judgment after he refused to certify two proposed amendments to the initiative petition (initiative) pursuant to art. 48, The Initiative, V, § 2, of the Amendments to the Constitution of the Commonwealth, as amended by art. 81, § 3, of the Amendments.


OVERVIEW: The initiative had been certified as meeting the initiative requirements by the attorney general. While preparing the summary, the attorney general alerted the sponsors that the initiative changed a portion of the term limits law passed through an initiative process. The sponsors collected the signatures and subsequently submitted two perfecting amendments to the attorney general. The attorney general refused to certify either of the amendments. The court ruled that when it reviewed proposed perfecting amendments that it should accept any reasonable judgment expressed by the attorney general. The court found that it was reasonable for the attorney general to reject the amendment regarding the inspector general's oversight of the commissioner of veterans' services because the removal was a material change of the initiative's substance. It was reasonable to reject the second proposed amendment that restored the reference to state senators from the term limits compensation law because it affected an entire branch of the government. The court adopted the opinion of the justices of the supreme judicial court that the initiative was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court declared that the attorney general's rejection of the proposed amendments to the initiative was within the reasonable exercise of his duties and that the initiative was unconstitutional.

 

62 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Benson v. Benson


SJC-06519


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


422 Mass. 698; 664 N.E.2d 838; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 119


January 11, 1996, Argued  
May 17, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Essex. Complaint for divorce filed in the Essex Division of the Probate and Family Court Department on April 17, 1984. A complaint for modification, filed on January 14, 1987, was heard by Edward J. Rockett, J. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both the father and mother appealed from the Essex Division of the Probate and Family Court Department (Massachusetts), which increased the father's child support obligations and suspended his spousal support obligations. The court granted the mother's application for direct appellate review.


OVERVIEW: The lower court granted custody of the children to the father and suspended his child support obligation. The father argued that this made the support issues moot. The mother countered that the support issues were not moot because the current custody arrangement was temporary and in place to help the mother overcome an alleged substance abuse problem. The court held that, because the amended temporary order continued to suspend the child support obligations and continued to grant the father sole legal and physical custody of the children, it rendered the child support issues moot. The mother sought to repudiate the divorce agreement in respect to both the cohabitation clause and to its evident intent to give separate consideration to her and her children's support. At the same time, she disagreed with the probate judge's independent evaluation of her needs because she did not like his conclusion that she earned sufficient money, so that the father was not required to pay her alimony at the present time. The court affirmed the suspension of the spousal support payments whether by looking to the intent of the parties or by virtue of an independent examination of the mother's needs.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the Probate and Family Court is affirmed.

Gray v. Commissioner of Revenue


SJC-07053


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


422 Mass. 666; 665 N.E.2d 17; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 118


January 11, 1996, Argued  
May 16, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Hampden. Civil action commenced in the Hampden Division of the Probate and Family Court Department on September 20, 1993. A complaint for contempt, filed on June 6, 1994, was heard by David G. Sacks, J., and the case was reported to the Appeals Court. The Supreme Judicial Court transferred the case on its own initiative.



DISPOSITION: Report discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff obligor appealed from a judgment of the Hampden Division of the Probate and Family Court Department (Massachusetts), which allowed defendant department of revenue to seize obligor's bank and credit union accounts to satisfy child support arrearages.


OVERVIEW: Obligor claimed that department of revenue's seizure of his bank and credit union accounts to pay child support arrearages constituted contempt of court and violated his rights to due process. Obligor asked the trial court to hold department of revenue in contempt of court, order to return his assets, and grant declaratory relief. The appellate court transferred the case. The court held that department of revenue had the authority to seize property of a child support obligor in satisfaction of child support arrearages, where the obligor was repaying the arrearages at a rate ordered by a probate court judge. The legislature did not violate Mass. Decl. Rts. art. 30 by restricting the probate court's power to levy under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 209C, or violate obligor's due process rights under Mass. Decl. Rts. art. 10. The report to the appellate court was therefore discharged.


OUTCOME: The appellate report that was made to the appellate court was discharged by the court.

 

64 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


McSweeney v. City of Cambridge


SJC-06968


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


422 Mass. 648; 665 N.E.2d 11; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 113


January 9, 1996, Argued  
May 15, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Middlesex. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on November 18, 1994. The case was heard by Wendie I. Gershengorn, J., on a motion to dismiss. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city council candidate sued defendant city and its election commissioners, disputing the method by which a vacancy on the city council had been filled. He asked that either he be declared elected to the vacant seat, that the ballots cast in the election be recounted by a different method, or that a new election be held. The Superior Court (Massachusetts) granted defendants' motion to dismiss. The candidate appealed.


OVERVIEW: The city had a proportional representation or preferential voting method for electing city council members, authorized as Plan E by Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 43, § 115. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 54A, § 13 prescribed that a vacancy must be filled for the remainder of the unexpired term by a public recount of the ballots credited at the end of the original count to the candidate elected thereby whose place had become vacant. The scheme looked to the voters of the vacating councilor to determine who would be the successor. Under the scheme, the candidate who stood twelfth in the election was selected, rather than plaintiff, the candidate who stood tenth. The court upheld the dismissal. Insofar as each voter's right to an equal voice in the electoral process was concerned, a scheme for filling vacancies need only meet rational basis review. Under this review, the court asked whether the means chosen was rationally related to the legitimate goal of allowing those who voted for the councilor whose seat was vacated to choose the successor. The court concluded that § 13 was a reasonable means for achieving a legitimate governmental goal and that it survived rational basis review.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the judgment of the trial court.

 

65 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Tolentino


SJC-06966


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


422 Mass. 515; 663 N.E.2d 846; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 87


March 7, 1996, Argued  
April 22, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Essex. Complaint received and sworn to in the Salem Division of the District Court Department on June 9, 1993. On transfer to the jury session of the Peabody Division, a motion to dismiss the jury venire was heard by Robert E. Hayes, J., and the case was tried before him. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the decision of the Peabody District Court (Massachusetts), which denied his motion to dismiss the jury venire as failing to represent the ethnic composition of the county and convicted him of possession of cocaine with intent to distribute within 1,000 feet of a school.


OVERVIEW: Charges of possession of cocaine with intent to distribute within 1,000 feet of a school were brought against defendant, and he brought a motion to dismiss the jury venire and alleged that it did not represent the ethnic composition of the county. The lower court denied defendant's motion and the court affirmed. The court found that the only real evidence presented by defendant in support of his allegation was an affidavit by his attorney, and that this evidence was insufficient to support his motion. The court held that defendant did not make an adequate showing of his prima facia case of unconstitutional jury selection. The court found that defendant failed to establish that Hispanics were not fairly represented in venires in the county in proportion to their numbers in the community. The court concluded that the affidavit did not establish that any possible underrepresentation of Hispanics in his jury venire was due to systematic exclusion. The court noted that the issue of underrepresentation should be carefully looked at, but that the motion was insufficient to support defendant's allegation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's denial of defendant motion to dismiss the jury venire and held that there was not sufficient evidence produced to support the allegations that the jury venire did not represent the ethnic composition of the county.

Commonwealth v. White


SJC-06336


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


422 Mass. 487; 663 N.E.2d 834; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 82


November 8, 1995, Argued  
April 18, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Middlesex. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on December 24, 1991. The cases were tried before Catherine A. White, J., and a motion for a new trial was considered by Nolan, J., in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Judicial Court of Suffolk County (Massachusetts) convicted defendants of murder in the first degree and armed assault in a dwelling. The Commonwealth tried the first degree murder charges on theories of joint venture felony-murder, joint venture murder with deliberate premeditation, and joint venture murder with extreme atrocity or cruelty. The defendants brought appeals.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the Commonwealth's case against defendants was sufficient to warrant the jury verdicts. The court further held that the police officers had reasonable suspicion to stop defendant's vehicle because the vehicle matched the description of witnesses. The court held that the subsequent warrantless search of the vehicle was properly upheld under the automobile exception to the warrant requirement. The court rejected one defendant's contention that he was denied ineffective assistance of counsel because his counsel failed to impeach a police officer's testimony of what defendant was wearing when he was arrested. The court held that the failure to impeach, on a full and reasonable assessment of the trial record, would not have played an important role in the jury's deliberations. Finally, the court held that the use at trial of the telephone number that one defendant called during his booking did not violate the defendant's constitutional rights under the Fifth Amendment to the United States Constitution. The court held that the suppression of the response to the routine booking question was not required because it was not obtained by compulsion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions.

Commonwealth v. Johnson


SJC-06255


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


422 Mass. 420; 663 N.E.2d 559; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 76


December 5, 1995, Argued  
April 9, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Middlesex. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on March 5, 1992. The cases were tried before Robert A. Barton, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court Department, Middlesex County (Massachusetts) that convicted him of murder in the first degree, armed assault with intent to murder, and two indictments charging assault and battery by means of a dangerous weapon.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his convictions for first-degree murder, armed assault with intent to murder, and assault and battery with a dangerous weapon. Defendant claimed the trial court erred in refusing to instruct the jury on lack of criminal responsibility, in its malice instruction, and in denying his motion to suppress voluntary statements made at the police station. The court affirmed defendant's convictions. The court found that the trial judge properly refused the instruction on the issue of criminal responsibility because there was no evidence that defendant suffered from a prior mental illness and the record revealed that he was calm and lucid before and after the crimes. The court held that the trial judge properly instructed the jury regarding the three elements for malice, thus his inclusion of an extra instruction regarding selfish motive did not constitute reversible error. The court held that the delay between defendant's arrest and the time he was booked and advised of his right to make a telephone call under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 276, § 33A and Miranda rights was not designed to gain inculpatory information but to allow the officers involved to be present at booking.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for murder in the first degree, armed assault with intent to murder, and two indictments charging assault and battery by means of a dangerous weapon.

 

68 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Corbett


SJC-06963


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


422 Mass. 391; 663 N.E.2d 259; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 69


March 7, 1996, Argued  
April 4, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Middlesex. Complaint received and sworn to in the Somerville Division of the District Court Department on September 19, 1994. The case was heard by Brian R. Merrick, J. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by the Somerville Division of the District Court Department (Massachusetts) of a third offense of operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor (DUI), in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 24(1)(a)(1) (1994). Defendant contended that his conviction was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. art. 1, § 9, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and Mass. Const. Declaration of Rights arts. 12 and 24.


OVERVIEW: The court, on its own initiative transferred the case from the appellate court. This was defendant's third conviction for DUI. The second time defendant was charged with DUI, he was not charged as a second offender because the second offense was not within six years of the first as required by Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 24(1)(a)(1) (1992 ed.). On defendant's third offense, ch. 90, § 24(1)(a)(1) (1994) had been enacted, which extended the reach-back for a second offense to 10 years and made the third offense a felony. Defendant was convicted of a third DUI and given prison time. In affirming defendant's conviction, the court ruled that: (1) his third-time conviction did not violate constitutional protections against ex post facto laws because his prior offenses only related to the punishment he received for the current offense: (2) his due process rights were not violated because he had fair warning that if he violated the new statute, his punishment could be enhanced by his prior convictions; and (3) it was not unconstitutional to treat defendant's prior two misdemeanor convictions as "like offenses" because the elements of each underlying criminal act remained the same.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for a third offense of operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor.

Volin v. Board of Pub. Accountancy


SJC-06892


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


422 Mass. 175; 661 N.E.2d 639; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 41


November 8, 1995, Argued  
February 26, 1996, Decided
