DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff accountant sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court Department (Massachusetts) that granted both the accountant's and defendant's, Board of Public Accountancy (board), motions for summary judgment in part in the accountant's action for declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: The accountant sought a declaratory judgment challenging the authority of the board under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 112, § 87D (1994 ed.) that restricted certain advertising practices and professional activities of unlicensed accountants. The trial court declared that unlicensed accountants could hold themselves out publicly as accountants but could not use advertising which contained any affiliation with the National Society of Public Accountants and certain other restrictions. In affirming, the court held that Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 112, § 87D did not unconstitutionally infringe on the accountant's free speech rights as guaranteed by the U.S. Const. art. 16 and the First Amendment. The court further found that Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 112, § 87D directly advanced the government's interest by guarding against the public's confusion of licensed and unlicensed accountants and that there was a "reasonable fit" between the government's interest and the restrictions of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the declaratory judgment issued by the lower court.

 

70 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


G.E.B. v. S.R.W.


SJC-06777


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


422 Mass. 158; 661 N.E.2d 646; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 42


November 6, 1995, Argued  
February 26, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Plymouth. Civil action commenced in the Plymouth Division of the Probate and Family Court Department on August 2, 1990. The case was heard by Edward W. Farrell, J. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of paternity is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mother filed a paternity action for her child against defendant putative father. He filed a motion to dismiss arguing that a previous declaratory action and a money settlement in exchange for her assent to a stipulation that he was not the child's father barred the action. The Probate and Family Court Department (Massachusetts) denied the motion and entered a judgment of paternity for the child. The putative father appealed.


OVERVIEW: The putative father alleged that the trial judge erred in not barring the action, in admitting evidence of a genetic marker test, and in awarding support and counsel fees to the child. On direct appellate review, the court held that even if the mother had been properly acting on behalf of the child, the settlement agreement was subject to disaffirmance by the child who was not a party to the settlement and there was no res judicata effect. There was no impairment of contract when the state law, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 209C, § 22(d), authorized the child to act and it was constitutional. The mother was not a legal representative of the child in the first action, so the child cannot be barred from pursuing her legal rights and there was no detrimental reliance, estoppel, or laches. The court found that a letter from the putative father to the mother was relevant as either to show that she considered him to be the father or to explain why he started visiting the child and was not excluded as hearsay. The putative father's claim of error as to the child support was premature because it was a temporary order. There was no statutory authorization for the child to be awarded attorney's fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of paternity and denied the child's requests for costs associated with the appeal. The court allowed the child's motion to strike a part, volume VII, of the record appendix.

Hosford v. School Comm.


SJC-06888


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


421 Mass. 708; 659 N.E.2d 1178; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 16


December 5, 1995, Argued  
January 18, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Barnstable. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on November 20, 1989. The case was heard by Elizabeth J. Dolan, J., and George C. Keady, Jr., J., on motions for summary judgment. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff teacher sought review of an order from the Superior Court Department (Massachusetts), which granted defendant school board's motion for summary judgment in the teacher's action seeking reinstatement of her special education teaching position and back pay on the grounds that the teacher's termination was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The teacher was teaching her special education students about words with more than one meaning. In the last few minutes of class, some of the students suggested various obscene words. The teacher explained that the words were not to be used in school, but discussed the words' different meanings. When the school board found out about the use of obscenities in class, the teacher was suspended, and was not rehired for the next school year. The teacher filed an action against the school board and sought reinstatement and back pay because her suspension and failure to be rehired was a violation her right to free speech under the First Amendment and the state declaration of rights. The school board filed a motion for summary judgment, which the trial court granted. The teacher sought review, and the court transferred the case from the appellate level on its own motion. The court found that the school board disciplinary action against the teacher for one brief class segment was a violation of the teacher's rights, that the teacher was entitled to reinstatement of one year of an untenured teaching position and any damages she suffered because of the school board's failure to rehire her.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's grant of summary judgment to the school board.

Fifty-One Hispanic Residents v. School Comm. of Chelsea


SJC-06798


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


421 Mass. 598; 659 N.E.2d 277; 1996 Mass. LEXIS 4


September 11, 1995, Argued  
January 5, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on November 23, 1988. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on June 13, 1989. The second case was transferred to the Superior Court Department by Abrams, J., where, on April 2, 1992, it was consolidated with the earlier case for trial. The cases were heard by Peter M. Lauriat, J., on motions for summary judgment, and, upon motion of the joint plaintiffs, the cases were reported to the Appeals Court. The Supreme Judicial Court granted a request for direct review.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, fifty-one Hispanic individuals, sought review of the judgment of the Appeals Court (Massachusetts), which found that an agreement entered into by appellee public school system with a public university, whereby the university would participate in managing the public school system for a period not exceeding 10 years, did not violate Massachusetts' anti-aid amendment, Mass. Const. art. 103, § 2.


OVERVIEW: Appellee, a Massachusetts public school system's school committee, entered into an agreement with a Massachusetts public university whereby the university would participate in managing the public school system for a period not exceeding 10 years. The governor of Massachusetts signed an enabling act into law that authorized the agreement. The agreement had been initiated because the school system offered unacceptably low levels of educational opportunity and the university was uniquely qualified to help appellee make significant improvements in the educational opportunities offered. Appellants, fifty-one Hispanic individuals, alleged that the agreement violated the state's anti-aid amendment, Mass. Const. art. 103, § 2. The judgment of the court below, which found the agreement to be constitutional, was affirmed. The court found that appellee had retained control of the public schools, directly or through the university, which was a public agent authorized by the Commonwealth. Therefore, the arrangement between appellee and the university did not offend the anti-aid amendment.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court below, which found that an agreement entered into by appellee public school system with a Massachusetts public university, whereby the university would participate in managing the public school system for a period not exceeding 10 years, did not violate Massachusetts' anti-aid amendment, was affirmed.

 

73 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Luk v. Commonwealth


SJC-06847


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


421 Mass. 415; 658 N.E.2d 664; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 455


September 11, 1995, Argued  
November 27, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on March 1, 1995. The case was heard by O'Connor, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Supreme Judicial Court for the County of Suffolk (Massachusetts), which denied her motion to dismiss plaintiff Commonwealth's complaint alleging that she operated a motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 24(1)(a)(1) (1994).


OVERVIEW: Defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint alleging that she operated a motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 24(1)(a)(1) (1994) was denied by the trial court. On appeal, the court held that the Commonwealth's earlier suspension of defendant's drivers license for refusing to submit to a breath test, pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 24(1)(f)(1), did not render the criminal charge a violation of defendant's Double Jeopardy Clause protections. The administrative license suspension was a reasonable sanction primarily designed to promote public safety, and not "punishment" for purposes of attachment of double jeopardy. Therefore, defendant failed to show that multiple punishments arose. Further, the § 24(1)(a)(1) offense and the refusal to submit to a breath test violation, pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 24(1)(g), were not the same offense for purposes of double jeopardy. The requirement that defendant submit to a breath test was only an element of § 24(1)(g), and separate sanctions were authorized for the offenses. Double jeopardy did not preclude punishment for two separate offenses.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of defendant's motion to dismiss the Commonwealth's complaint alleging that she operated a motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor.

 

74 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Matsos


SJC-06882


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


421 Mass. 391; 657 N.E.2d 467; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 452


October 4, 1995, Argued  
November 20, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Essex. Complaint received and sworn to in the Salem Division of the District Court Department on March 16, 1993. On transfer to the jury session of the Peabody Division, the case was tried before Robert E. Hayes, J. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment from the District Court (Massachusetts), which convicted him under the stalking statute, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 265, § 43 (1992).


OVERVIEW: Defendant wrote many letters to the victim that were sexually explicit and threatening and which detailed events of her life of which defendant should not have had any knowledge. Defendant was charged with stalking the victim in violation of § 43. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's conviction. The court concluded that the amount and content of the letters that defendant sent to the victim allowed the jury to conclude that defendant intended to place the victim in fear of imminent bodily injury and that the victim was afraid of defendant. After defendant's conviction, § 43 was found to be unconstitutionally vague. Because defendant did not challenge the constitutionality of § 43 at his trial, the court could only consider whether defendant's conviction under the statute as enacted, created a substantial risk of a miscarriage of justice. Regardless of the vagueness of portions of the statute, defendant's conduct was clearly prohibited harassment. The statute gave the defendant fair notice that his repeated, obsessive behavior, which interfered with the victim's personal and professional lives, was prohibited.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction under the stalking statute.

Commonwealth v. Koney


SJC-06849


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


421 Mass. 295; 657 N.E.2d 210; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 374


September 11, 1995, Argued  
November 9, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Essex. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on October 2, 1991. The case was tried before Margot Botsford, J. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was found guilty of homicide by motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor, operating the same having been previously convicted of a like offense, operating a motor vehicle after his license to do so had been suspended or revoked, leaving the scene of an accident after causing personal injury and property damage, and failing to stop for a police officer. The commonwealth applied for direct appellate review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was driving a car down a street when he hit a man who had just parked his car on the side of the road. Defendant did not stop until the police pursued him. Once stopped, the police determined that he was intoxicated and arrested him. Defendant presented evidence that he was not intoxicated and that his blood alcohol content was never tested. The jury was instructed with reference to the lack of such testing and defense counsel objected to the charge. The particular instruction was declared unconstitutional in a decision after defendant was convicted. Defendant argued that this decision should be applied retroactively. The court found that the error in giving the instruction was not harmless because it raised an improper inference about defendant's allegedly intoxicate state. Thus, the court reversed his convictions for homicide by a motor vehicle and operating a motor vehicle while under the influence. Defendant's motion for a finding of not guilty should have been allowed on the subsequent offense charge because the commonwealth did not prove that defendant was actually the same person as the defendant named in the three prior convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of operating a motor vehicle after defendant's license had been revoked, leaving the scene of an accident, and failing to stop for a police officer. The court reversed the judgment of homicide by motor vehicle and operating the same while under the influence and remanded for further proceedings. The court also reversed the judgment of being a subsequent offender and directed a judgment to be entered for defendant.

Commonwealth v. D'Agostino


SJC-06894


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


421 Mass. 281; 657 N.E.2d 217; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 376


September 11, 1995, Argued  
November 9, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Essex. Complaint received and sworn to in the Lynn Division of the District Court Department on June 28, 1991. On transfer to the jury session of the Peabody Division, A pretrial motion to suppress evidence was heard by Domenic J.F. Russo, J., and the case was tried before him. After the review by the Appeals Court, the Supreme Judicial Court granted leave to obtain further appellate review.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction must be reversed and the verdict set aside.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the District Court Department (Massachusetts), which convicted him of operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor, in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 24(1)(a)(1) (1994).


OVERVIEW: During defendant's trial for operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor, the two arresting officers testified that defendant was unsteady on his feet, that his eyes were "glossy" and "watery," that his breath had a strong odor of alcohol, that and his speech was slurred. Both officers further testified that they formed the opinion that defendant was under the influence of alcohol. An emergency medical technician who observed defendant at the police station also testified that he thought that defendant was intoxicated. The officers were also allowed to testify that defendant did not want to take a field sobriety test. The lower court judge later instructed the jury that there was to be no speculation as to why the test was not administered, pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 24(1)(e). There was no objection to the instruction and defendant was convicted. The court reversed the conviction, holding that defendant was entitled to claim the retroactive benefit of a decision that ruled that the instruction given by the judge unconstitutionally compelled him to furnish testimonial evidence against himself.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, which convicted defendant of operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor, and set aside the verdict.

 

77 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Adams


SJC-06843


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


421 Mass. 289; 657 N.E.2d 455; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 377


September 11, 1995, Argued  
November 9, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Essex. Complaint received and sworn to in the Lynn Division of the District Court Department on February 15, 1992. On transfer to the jury session of the Peabody Division, the case was heard by Robert E. Hayes, J. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Peabody District Court (Massachusetts) after he was convicted of operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor, under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 24 (1)(a)(1) (1994), and operating a motor vehicle after suspension of his license to operate, under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 23 (1994).


OVERVIEW: Two cars collided on a highway. The driver of one of the vehicles indicated to police that the accident had been caused by the manner in which the other vehicle was being driven. The police concluded that defendant had operated the second vehicle under the influence of intoxicating liquor. On appeal from his conviction, defendant argued that the Commonwealth's evidence was insufficient to warrant a finding that he was the operator of the second vehicle involved in the accident. The court found that the finding was warranted, as defendant's admission that he had been driving the vehicle was adequately corroborated by the other driver's statement to police. The court also rejected defendant's argument that he was entitled to retroactive application of a rule that a jury was not to be instructed on the availability of blood alcohol testing when no evidence of such testing was presented at trial. The court based its holding on the fact that an opinion had been presented to the legislature, indicating that a law to allow the admission into evidence of a defendant's refusal to submit to a blood alcohol test would have violated the self-incrimination provision of Mass. Const. art. 12.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction.

Nuclear Metals v. Low-Level Radioactive Waste Management Bd.


SJC-06638


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


421 Mass. 196; 656 N.E.2d 563; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 363; 41 ERC (BNA) 2093


January 9, 1995, Argued  
October 17, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on June 16, 1992. The case was heard by J. Harold Flannery, J., on a statement of agreed facts. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff manufacturer and defendant, a low-level radioactive waste management board, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court (Massachusetts). The judgment declared defendant's assessment against plaintiff under Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 111H, §§ 4A, B (1994), to be a valid fee rather than an unconstitutional charge, but vacated defendant's computation of the assessment on the ground that defendant lacked jurisdiction to hear the appeal.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff manufacturer filed an action against defendant, low-level radioactive waste management board, seeking a determination that an assessment under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 111H, §§ 4A, B (1994), and Mass. Regs. Code tit. 845, § 4.00 (1992), was unlawful and challenging the amount of the assessment. The trial court ruled that the assessment was a valid fee but vacated defendant's decision regarding computation of the assessment. The court affirmed in part and vacated in part the judgment. The court held that plaintiff failed to satisfy its burden of proving that the assessment was not a lawful fee. The court rejected plaintiff's argument that the term "assessment" necessarily meant that a "tax" rather than a "fee" had been imposed. The court held that defendant provided a service for which it could legitimately exact a fee. The court held that under the terms of the Low-Level Radioactive Waste Policy Amendments Act of 1985, 42 U.S.C.S. § 2021a et seq. (1988), plaintiff could not manage the disposal of its waste without reliance on the regulatory services performed by defendant. The court held that defendant's services provided plaintiff a "sufficiently particularized" benefit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in part regarding the validity of the assessment by defendant, low-level radioactive waste management board, against plaintiff manufacturer but vacated and remanded the part of the judgment that held that defendant lacked jurisdiction to hear plaintiff's appeal regarding the computation of the assessment. The court held that plaintiff should shoulder costs associated with its radioactive waste.

 

79 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Doe v. Superintendent of Sch.


SJC-06746


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


421 Mass. 117; 653 N.E.2d 1088; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 332


March 7, 1995, Argued  
August 11, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Worcester. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on April 29, 1994. The case was heard by Barbara A. Lenk, J. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff student appealed a judgment from the Superior Court Department (Massachusetts), which upheld her expulsion from school for one year for possessing a lipstick case containing a one and one-quarter inch blade. Defendant school district argued that the case was moot since the student was back in school. The student argued that defendants abused their discretion in expelling her and violated her constitutional right to an education.


OVERVIEW: A student was expelled for one year for bringing a knife to school. The student had a history of suicide attempts and on the day she brought the lipstick case containing a knife to school, her wrist was bandaged from her third attempt at suicide. Through her mother, the student brought an action contending that if the Education Reform Act, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 71, § 37H applied to that kind of knife that it was unconstitutionally vague. In affirming the judgment of the superior court, the court held that the student's case was not moot because she had a real and substantial stake in the outcome of her case. The principal of the school did not abuse his discretion because once he decided that he could not say that the student did not pose a risk of harm to other students, he had no discretion but to expel the student. The legislature did not intend to limit the knives that children could not bring to school to those found in Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 269, § 10 (b) and there was no constitutional right to education in Massachusetts. The student's substantive rights to due process had not been violated and, § 37H was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court, which upheld the student's expulsion from school for one year for possessing a lipstick case containing a one and one-quarter inch blade.

Neff v. Commissioner of the Dep't of Indus. Accidents


SJC-06612


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


421 Mass. 70; 653 N.E.2d 556; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 331


December 5, 1994, Argued  
August 9, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on June 13, 1994. The case was reported by Greaney, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff claimant sought declaratory and injunctive relief in the Supreme Judicial Court for Suffolk County (Massachusetts), which did not enter a judgment on her appeal from a decision of defendant Commissioner of the Department of Industrial Accidents denying the claimant's motion for a waiver of the fee for an impartial medical examination required by Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 152, § 11A(2). The claimant challenged the validity of § 11A(2).


OVERVIEW: The claimant had applied for workers' compensation benefits and had been denied by the Department. The claimant had reached the point in the appeals process in which an impartial medical examination was required. The claimant's claim that she was indigent was not refuted by the Department. The court remanded the case to the county court. The court held as follows: 1) the legislature did not intend to force a claimant out of the workers' compensation dispute process merely because the claimant was indigent and could not pay for an impartial medical examination, especially since an indigent claimant could apply for a fee waiver at every other stage where a fee was required; 2) forcing a claimant out of the dispute process would have defeated the purpose of the workers' compensation act and would have been inconsistent with express provisions for fee waivers for indigency; and 3) the workers' compensation statute implicitly conferred on the Department the authority to grant waivers of the impartial medical examination fee required by § 11A, for indigent claimants, even those represented by attorneys.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the case to the county court for further proceedings.

Trigones v. Attorney Gen.


SJC-06574


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


420 Mass. 859; 652 N.E.2d 893; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 319

July 26, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on November 29, 1993. The case was heard by Lynch, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a defendant in a criminal proceeding, appealed a decision of the Supreme Judicial Court for the County of Suffolk (Massachusetts), which entered judgment against him in his declaratory judgment action challenging the constitutionality of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 278, § 33E (1992).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was convicted of murder in the first degree and sentenced to life imprisonment without parole. His motions for new trial were denied, and a single justice of the court, acting as "gatekeeper" under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 278, § 33E remanded to the superior court for an evidentiary hearing to determine whether the motion for new trial raised any substantial issues which would have justified review by the full court. The superior court judge denied the motion, and plaintiff appealed. A second single justice, acting as gatekeeper, denied the application because plaintiff had failed to raise any new and substantial question which ought to have been determined by the full court. Plaintiff filed a complaint for declaratory relief challenging the constitutionality of § 33E, which was denied, and he appealed. The court affirmed, rejecting plaintiff's arguments that the "gatekeeper" provision violated his due process and equal protection rights, concluding that the gatekeeper provision merely screened out appeals that lack merit by requiring a single justice to give approval for a full court review.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment against plaintiff in his declaratory judgment action challenging the constitutionality of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 278, § 33E (1992).

Curtis v. School Comm.


SJC-06684


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


420 Mass. 749; 652 N.E.2d 580; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 302; 52 A.L.R.5th 877


March 7, 1995, Argued  
July 17, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Barnstable.

Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on May 22, 1992.

The case was heard by Gerald F. O'Neill, Jr., J., on a motion for summary judgment.

The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant school officials established a program of condom availability. Plaintiffs, students and parents of students, requested declaratory relief that the program violated their right to familial privacy, and their right to the free exercise of religion guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. I. The Superior Court Department (Massachusetts) granted summary judgment for the school officials. The students and parents of students appealed.


OVERVIEW: The school district established a program of condom distribution at the junior and senior high school. No classroom participation was required and the students could request the condoms free with counseling or obtain them from vending machines for a fee. The students were free to participate in the program and no penalty or disciplinary action ensued if a student did not participate. The students and parents of students filed an action for declaratory and injunctive relief based on a violation of their constitutional rights. The trial court granted summary judgment for the school officials. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that (1) the mere exposure to the condom program offered at school did not amount to unconstitutional interference with parental liberties without some compulsory aspect to the program; (2) neither an opt-out nor parental notification provision was required by the federal constitution; (3) although the program might offend the religious sensibilities of the students and parents of students, mere exposure to the program without compulsion did not amount to a violation of the free exercise clause of the First Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment in favor of the school officials because the condom availability program did not violate the students' and parents of students' right to familial privacy and free exercise clause of the First Amendment.

Automobile Insurers Bureau v. Commissioner of Ins.


SJC-06803


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


420 Mass. 599; 650 N.E.2d 1234; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 290


March 7, 1995, Argued  
June 19, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on December 22, 1994. The case was reported by Abrams, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, insurance company association and member companies (association), brought an action challenging a decision of defendant state commissioner of insurance (commissioner), which established private passenger motor vehicle insurance rates for 1995.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner determined that competition was insufficient to assure that private passenger automobile insurance rates would not be excessive and, thus, invoked the procedures of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 175, § 113B, to fix and establish the rates. The court affirmed the commissioner's decision, which resulted in an over-all rate reduction of 6.1 percent. The court found that the commissioner properly reduced loss pure premiums for bodily injury coverage to reflect anticipated savings from the 1994 seat belt law. The court rejected the association's arguments that the reduction was not authorized, that the model used to establish the amount of the reduction was faulty, and that the inputs used in applying the model were improper. The court also found that the commissioner properly concluded that an increase in premium rates was not warranted by a regulatory change in the use of referral shops and the deregulation of the towing industry. Finally, the court concluded that the association did not satisfy its burden of proving that the rates were unconstitutionally confiscatory.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the commissioner's decision that established private passenger motor vehicle insurance rates.

Frizado v. Frizado


SJC-06729


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


420 Mass. 592; 651 N.E.2d 1206; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 284


April 4, 1995, Argued  
June 15, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on September 26, 1994. The case was heard by Nolan, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of an order of the trial court (Massachusetts), which denied appellant's motion to dismiss a complaint filed by appellee wife and an ex parte order issued by the trial court pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 209A.


OVERVIEW: Appellant husband challenged the constitutionality of the trial court's order that issued a preliminary and permanent abuse prevention order pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 209A, and the trial court's denial of appellant's motion to dismiss appellee former wife's complaint for spousal abuse. The court affirmed and held that although a trial court other than the issuing trial court vacated the orders upon motion of appellant, the issue was not moot because the court found that records containing the issuance of the order might have an adverse effect on appellant in the future. The court held that ch. 209A did not violate appellant's right to a jury trial under Mass. Const. Declaration of Rights art. XII. The court reasoned that an order that affected access to property pursuant to ch. 209A was not punitive in purpose. The court found no right to a jury trial pursuant to Mass. Const. Declarations of Rights art. XV in a ch. 209A action, because there was no right to a jury trial in domestic abuse cases prior to 1780. The court held that the mere failure of appellant to testify in a ch. 209A hearing was insufficient in itself to warrant the issuance of an abuse prevention order.


OUTCOME: An order that issued a preliminary and permanent abuse prevention order was affirmed because appellant husband did not have a right to a jury trial in a hearing on the issuance of the order. The court found that the abuse prevention statute was not punitive. Appellant did not have a state constitutional right to a jury trial in an abuse prevention action because no such right was available when the state constitution was adopted.

 

85 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Arthur


SJC-06775


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


420 Mass. 535; 650 N.E.2d 787; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 281


May 3, 1995, Argued  
June 13, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Norfolk. Complaint received and sworn to in the Wrentham Division of the District Court Department on July 12, 1993. On transfer to the jury session of the Dedham Division, the case was tried before William T. Hogan, Jr., J. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Dedham Division of the District Court Department (Massachusetts) that convicted him of indecent exposure in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 272, § 53 (1992).


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conviction was based on the complaint of a mother and daughter that alleged that he pulled down his pants in front of them, revealing his pubic area. On appeal, defendant argued that the evidence was insufficient to warrant the conclusion that he had exposed his genitalia and that the statute under which he was convicted did not adequately apprise him that his actions could have subjected him to punishment. Upon review, the court reversed the trial court's judgment. The testimony concerning defendant's pulling down of the right side of his bathing suit or shorts was insufficient to support an inference by the jury beyond a reasonable doubt that his conduct at that point had necessarily exposed some portion of his genitalia. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 272, § 53 (1992) had not been construed by judicial decision to apply to an intentional exposure of the genital area or the pubic hair. Therefore, the court was unable to conclude that the term indecent exposure, or the decisional law of the commonwealth construing the term, would have adequately apprised defendant that the conduct in which he engaged could have subjected him to criminal sanctions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and held that the statute which defendant was convicted under did not adequately apprise him that the conduct in which he engaged could have subjected him to criminal sanctions.

Commonwealth v. Power


SJC-06495


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


420 Mass. 410; 650 N.E.2d 87; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 226; 23 Media L. Rep. 2006


December 6, 1994, Argued  
May 18, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied Power v. Massachusetts, 516 U.S. 1042, 116 S. Ct. 697, 133 L. Ed. 2d 655, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 16 (1996)
Related proceeding at Commonwealth v. Power, 2013 Mass. Super. LEXIS 111 (Mass. Super. Ct., July 31, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court on October 1, 1970. The cases were heard by Robert W. Banks, J. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The defendant, after being in hiding for many years following the commission of crimes by herself and her confederates, pleaded guilty to indictments for armed robbery and manslaughter. The defendant was granted leave to appeal from the portion of her sentence, instituted by the Superior Court, Suffolk County (Massachusetts), which placed a special condition on her probation that she not profit from the sale of her story to the news media.


OVERVIEW: The defendant requested that the court vacate the special condition that she not profit from the sale of her story on the grounds (1) that it violated her right to free expression under the U.S. Const. amend I, because it imposed a prior restraint on content-based speech, (2) that it unconstitutionally placed her in jeopardy for the actions of third persons, and (3) that it was unconstitutionally vague. The court rejected the arguments. Although the condition implicated her constitutional rights, it only barred the her ability to profit from the sale of her story, not her right to speak on the matter. In addition, a probation condition that was reasonably related to a goal of the justice system, such as deterrence, was valid, if it was reasonable. The impact of this condition on the defendant's rights was negligible in terms of its deterrence value. On the second claim, the defendant was not at risk from third parties telling parts of her story, because the prohibition was merely against her profiting from other's using the story. Lastly, the court held that, although a probation condition was required to be clear, the one in question was clear, so the point lacked merit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the probation condition placed on the defendant, prohibiting her from profiting from the sale of the story of her crimes and hiding to the media.

Drapkin v. Commissioner of Revenue


SJC-06466


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


420 Mass. 333; 649 N.E.2d 1094; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 225


October 6, 1994, Argued  
May 16, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Appeal from a decision of the Appellate Tax Board. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Decision of the Appellate Tax Board affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted plaintiff taxpayer's application for direct appellate review of a decision entered in favor of defendant Commissioner of Revenue by the Appellate Tax Board of Suffolk County (Massachusetts), which affirmed the commissioner's refusal to abate taxes paid by the taxpayer.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer was a 50 percent shareholder of two Subchapter S Massachusetts corporations and reported on his individual income tax return his share of the interest income from loans made by the two corporations as Part B income. Following an audit, the commissioner reclassified the interest income as Part A income, which was taxed at a higher rate. In addition, the commissioner required the taxpayer to deduct his share of the business expenses of the corporations from his Part B income and allowed him to deduct the expenses from interest income, Part A income, only to the extent that they exceeded the amount of the taxpayer's Part B income. The commissioner disallowed the taxpayer's applications for abatement. On direct appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the appellate tax board and held that: 1) the classification of interest income derived by the taxpayer through the corporations as Part A gross income did not violate Mass. Const. Declaration of Rights amend. art. 44; 2) the exception provided for pawnbrokers provided in Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 62, § 2(b)(1)(B) was constitutional; 3) the ordering of deductions provided in ch. 62, § 2(c) was also constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate tax board that was entered in favor of the commissioner.

Commonwealth v. Doe


SJC-06707

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with rape and other sexual offenses. The charges were resolved by an entry of nolle prosequi by the state. The district court denied the petition and sought to report questions concerning the petition to the appeals court. Defendant sought direct appellate review.

Defendant sought to have the records of the case sealed. R. Crim. P. 34 in effect at the time to report questions. However, the court decided to comment on the issues and found that the district court correctly dismissed defendant's petition because there was insufficient evidence of the reason for the nolle prosequi, an important factor to consider. The court also noted that the risk of damage to defendant's reputation and future prospects was more substantial than indicated by the district court.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the district court's report of questions concerning defendant's petition to seal his criminal records, but found that the district court properly dismissed the petition.

Massachusetts Community College Council v. Commonwealth


SJC-06695


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


420 Mass. 126; 649 N.E.2d 708; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 147


February 7, 1995, Argued  
April 19, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil actions commenced in the Superior Court Department, seven on April 5, 1991; one on April 8, 1991; one on April 9, 1991; one on November 14, 1991; three on November 19, 1991; two on December 18, 1991; one on March 10, 1992; and one on April 30, 1992. The cases were heard by Hiller B. Zobel, J., on a statement on agreed facts. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Review was granted of a decision of the Suffolk Superior Court (Massachusetts) that determined after a consolidated trial that defendant commonwealth's program for instituting a state employee pay furlough violated U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 cl. 1 as it was applied to plaintiffs, state community college faculty, staff, and their unions.


OVERVIEW: The unions challenged an employee furlough program instituted by the commonwealth to assist in reducing a large commonwealth deficit. The unions contended that the furlough program violated U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 largely on the basis that the program abrogated various collective bargaining agreements entered in December 1990. The superior court determined that the furlough provisions violated U.S. Const. art. I, §10 on their face, and the commonwealth's action was not justified under any circumstances. The court granted review and held that the matter required a determination on whether the furloughs were reasonable and necessary. The court held that the commonwealth's furloughs were not reasonable because the commonwealth could have foreseen the impending budget crisis when it entered into the union contracts in December 1990. The commonwealth's financial position had not worsened in the few months between the execution of the union contracts and the implementation of the furloughs in April 1991 and it was not reasonable to ignore the union contracts. The court did not address whether the action was necessary. The judgment of the superior court was vacated.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the superior court was vacated and a new judgment was entered declaring that the implementation of the furlough program impaired the obligation of the commonwealth to pay compensation pursuant to the respective collective bargaining agreements and was unconstitutional.

 

91 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Perini Corp. v. Comm'r of Revenue


SJC-06657


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


419 Mass. 763; 647 N.E.2d 52; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 121


January 10, 1995, Argued  
March 22, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on September 24, 1993. The case was reported by O'Connor, J.



DISPOSITION:  The court remanded to the trial court for the entry of a suitable declaration that the challenged tax provisions violated the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution, and for the entry of an order that the commissioner should decide the amount of the abatements to be made to the corporations. The court remanded the matter of attorney's fees to the single justice for further consideration.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporations filed an action in the Supreme Judicial Court for the County of Suffolk (Massachusetts) against defendant commissioner alleging that certain portions of Massachusetts' corporate excise statute violated the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution. A single justice of the court reserved and reported the case to the full court based on the pleadings and the statement of agreed facts.


OVERVIEW: The tax provisions at issue allowed a domestic corporation to deduct from its taxable net worth for the purposes of the state corporate excise tax the value of its domestic subsidiaries and allowed foreign corporation to deduct the value of its foreign subsidiaries for the same purpose. The court found that the tax provisions violated the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution. The court concluded that the provisions facially discriminated against interstate commerce because the provision drew distinctions solely on the basis of the domicil of the subsidiary, and treated a domestic corporation more favorably if it chose to acquire a domestic subsidiary, rather than a foreign subsidiary. Furthermore, the scheme imposed a heavier burden on domestic corporations who own foreign subsidiaries than on domestic corporations who own domestic subsidiaries. The court also found that the elimination of double taxation, a legitimate state interest, did not justify the provisions because they only eliminated double taxation for domestic corporations that owned only domestic subsidiaries. The problem of double taxation persisted for multi-state corporate families.


OUTCOME: The court remanded to the trial court for the entry of a suitable declaration that the challenged tax provisions violated the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution, and for the entry of an order that the commissioner should decide the amount of the abatements to be made to the corporations. The court remanded the matter of attorney's fees to the single justice for further consideration.

 

92 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. McGrail


SJC-06709


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


419 Mass. 774; 647 N.E.2d 712; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 120


January 10, 1995, Argued  
March 22, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Middlesex. Complaint received and sworn to in the Framingham Division of the District Court Department on March 5, 1991. On transfer to the jury session, the case was tried before Robert V. Greco, J., and a motion for a new trial was heard by him. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Framingham Division of the District Court Department (Massachusetts), which convicted him of the state's charges of operating under the influence of intoxicating liquor in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 24 (1990), and operating a motor vehicle negligently so as to endanger in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 10 (1992), and denied his motion for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped by a police officer after driving recklessly and refused to take field sobriety tests. He was charged with and later convicted of operating under the influence of intoxicating liquor in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 24 (1990), and operating a motor vehicle negligently so as to endanger in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 10 (1992), and found "responsible" for a civil marked lanes infraction in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 89, § 4A (1992). The trial court denied his motion for a new trial. On appeal, the court reversed the convictions and remanded for a new trial because it found that the trial court erred in allowing the state to use evidence of the fact that defendant had refused to take field sobriety tests. The court found that use of refusal evidence violated defendant's privilege against self-incrimination secured by Mass. Const. Declaration of Rights art. 12. The court also found that although there was ample other evidence with regard to defendant's intoxication, it could not conclude that the error in admitting refusal evidence was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions and remanded for a new trial because it found that the trial court erred in admitting evidence of defendant's refusal to take a field sobriety test. The court found that admitting refusal evidence violated defendant's privilege against self-incrimination and that its admittance was not harmless beyond a reasonable doubt.

 

93 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Tobias v. Secretary of the Commonwealth


SJC-06767


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


419 Mass. 665; 646 N.E.2d 1054; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 60


December 22, 1994, Argued  
March 9, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on December 6, 1994. The case was reported by Wilkins, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff voters filed an action against defendant Secretary of State (Secretary) alleging that the secretary's system applicable to voting machines and electronic voting systems failed to comply with the requirements of art. 48, General Provisions, III, Form of Ballot, of the Amendments to the Constitution of the Commonwealth as amended by art. 74 of the Amendments.


OVERVIEW: The voters filed an action against the Secretary, arguing that system applicable to voting machines and electronic voting systems failed to comply with certain constitutional amendments. The voters argued that no fair and concise summaries appeared on the ballots for the election as was required. The court held that the system developed by the Secretary for use in the state election for presentation to the voters of the attorney general's summaries of the ballot questions, by which the summaries were placed on sheets of paper not physically attached to, but cross-referenced on, documents or devices upon which votes were recorded, complied with the requirements of Article 48, General Provisions, Part III of the Amendments to the Constitution of the Commonwealth. The court found that when the Secretary determined that it was not feasible for the summaries to appear on voting machines, the preparation of separate sheets of paper containing the attorney general's various summaries and the furnishing of a sheet to each voter as he prepared to cast his vote by the use of such a machine fulfilled the basic purpose of having each voter capable of informing himself of the questions.


OUTCOME: The court held that the Secretary's voting system applicable to voting machines and electronic voting systems did not fail to comply with the constitutional requirements.

 

94 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


National Ass'n of Gov't Employees v. Commonwealth


SJC-06650


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


419 Mass. 448; 646 N.E.2d 106; 1995 Mass. LEXIS 31; 149 L.R.R.M. 2690


November 10, 1994, Argued  
February 9, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Middlesex. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on September 23, 1993. The case was heard by Peter M. Lauriat, J., on a statement of agreed facts. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the Commonwealth of Massachusetts (commonwealth), appealed the decision of the Superior Court Department, Middlesex County (Massachusetts), which declared a provision of the commonwealth's budget increasing health insurance premiums for certain union members unconstitutional, in a declaratory judgment action brought by plaintiffs, union, several of its officials, and two state employees.


OVERVIEW: The trial judge entered a declaration to that the line item at issue violated the collective bargaining agreement (CBA) between the parties. The commonwealth appealed. The court granted the commonwealth's application for direct appellate review. The court declared that the line item that reduced the commonwealth's contribution to 85 percent did not conflict with the collective bargaining agreements and therefore did not violate the contract clause. The challenged line item was a valid exercise by the legislature of its reserved power. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 32A, § 8 existed at the time the CBAs were made; the CBAs incorporated a mandate that the appropriation process determined the percentage of the commonwealth's contribution. Because the CBAs themselves necessarily contemplated changes in premium contributions through subsequent appropriation acts, there was no conflict between the line item and the CBAs. There was no impairment of the union's contractual rights and therefore no violation of the contract clause.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court that held that a line item that changed the commonwealth's contribution to employee health plans was unconstitutional and a violation of the contract clause.

 

95 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Epps v. Commonwealth


S-6338


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


419 Mass. 97; 642 N.E.2d 1026; 1994 Mass. LEXIS 624


October 4, 1994, Argued  
December 5, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on August 2, 1993. The case was considered by Nolan, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a civil action, plaintiff appealed the decision of the Supreme Judicial Court for the County of Suffolk (Massachusetts), which denied his petition under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 211, § 3 (1992) after he was convicted of possession with intent to distribute a class B controlled substance, and possession with intent to distribute a class B controlled substance within 1,000 feet of a school.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was charged in the trial court with possession with intent to distribute a class B controlled substance in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 94C, § 32A (1992), and possession with intent to distribute a class B controlled substance within 1,000 feet of a school in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 94C, § 32J (1992). He moved to strike from the complaint, which was denied. Plaintiff also moved for the dismissal of the complaint on the ground of unconstitutional selective enforcement of the school zone statute, which was denied. He then brought a civil petition for relief under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 211, § 3 (1992). On appeal, the court affirmed because the judge's action in sentencing on the school zone count contradicted any claim that the judge found plaintiff guilty of only simple possession. In addition, the denial of the motion to relief until after trial, and relief under the civil petition was not available as a matter of right.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of plaintiff's civil petition for relief following his convictions for possession with intent to distribute a class B controlled substance, and possession with intent to distribute a class B controlled substance within 1,000 feet of a school.

 

96 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Colin C.


W-6540


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


419 Mass. 54; 643 N.E.2d 19; 1994 Mass. LEXIS 622


September 13, 1994, Argued  
November 28, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Worcester. Complaints received and sworn to in the Worcester Division of the Juvenile Court Department on June 3, 1991, and July 9, 1991, respectively. On appeal to the jury session of that division, the case was tried before Elliott L. Zide, J. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a juvenile, challenged the judgment of the Worcester Division of the Juvenile Court Department (Massachusetts), which found him delinquent by reason of his guilt of three counts of indecent assault and battery on a child under the age of 14, six counts of rape of a child with force, and three counts of kidnapping.


OVERVIEW: A juvenile was charged with three counts of indecent assault and battery on a child under the age of 14, six counts of rape of a child with force, and three counts of kidnapping. A child psychiatrist and an expert on child abuse conducted physical examinations and clinical interviews of two alleged victims. At trial, the psychiatrist testified as an expert and testified generally about the effect of sexual abuse on children, and explained the psychological dimensions of disclosure. The expert also testified, over objection, that in her opinion the alleged victims had been sexually abused. The court reversed, finding that experts could not testify as to the issue of credibility and that the expert's opinion testimony that the alleged victims were sexually abused was tantamount to an expert opinion that the children's claims of sexual abuse were likely true.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments, set aside the verdicts against the juvenile, and remanded for a new trial.

 

97 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Connolly's Case


S-6533


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


418 Mass. 848; 642 N.E.2d 296; 1994 Mass. LEXIS 614


September 12, 1994, Argued  
November 16, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected November 22, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Appeal from a decision of the Industrial Accident Reviewing Board. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurer sought review of a decision of the Massachusetts Industrial Accident Reviewing Board, which reversed a decision that held that appellee employee, who was incarcerated, was not entitled to workers' compensation benefits after the effective date of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 152, § 8(2)(j).


OVERVIEW: The employee began receiving weekly compensation benefits for temporary total incapacity from the insurer. When the employee was later convicted of a felony and incarcerated, the insurer discontinued benefits. An administrative judge ordered the insurer to continue paying. While an administrative hearing was pending, the workers' compensation statute was amended to permit discontinuance of payments when an employee was incarcerated. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 152, § 8(2)(j). Based on the amendment, the insurer again stopped the payments. The administrative judge ruled that the employee was only entitled to payment up to the effective date of the amendment. The board reversed the administrative judge and ruled that § 8(2)(j) did not apply to claims arising out of injuries that occurred before the effective date. On appeal, the court reversed. As a procedural provision, § 8(2)(j) applied to personal injuries irrespective of the date of their occurrence. Applying § 8(2)(j) to claims based on injuries that arose before the effective date of the amendment raised no constitutional issue because an incarcerated employee had no vested right in benefits.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision that reversed a decision holding that the employee, who was incarcerated, was not entitled to workers' compensation benefits from the insurer after the effective date of a statute that permitted discontinuance of benefits if an employee was incarcerated.

 

98 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Zevitas


S-6413


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


418 Mass. 677; 639 N.E.2d 1076; 1994 Mass. LEXIS 504


March 8, 1994, Argued  
September 23, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on October 18, 1990. The cases were tried before Elbert Tuttle, J. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court (Massachusetts), which gave a jury instruction pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 24(1)(e) regarding defendant's failure to take a blood alcohol test and found him guilty of three charges related to the reckless use of an automobile while under the influence of intoxicating liquor.


OVERVIEW: Defendant struck and killed the victim while the victim was attempting to push his disabled vehicle. Defendant claimed that he did not see disabled vehicle. The troopers on the scene testified that defendant was under the influence of alcohol because his breath had a strong odor of alcohol, his eyes were bloodshot and glassy, he was unsteady on his feet, and his speech was slurred. On appeal, the court concluded that the evidence was sufficient to support the indictments. At the close of the commonwealth's case, a rational trier of fact could have found defendant guilty; therefore, defendant's motions for required findings of not guilty were properly denied. No evidence was presented that defendant refused to submit to a blood alcohol test; however, the trial court's instruction under §24(1)(e) tended to have the same affect as refusal evidence. Consequently, § 24(1)(e) unconstitutionally compelled defendant to furnish evidence against himself. Defendant was entitled to a new trial because the jury instruction likely caused the jury to conclude that defendant refused to submit to the test and to impermissibly use that evidence against defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant for reckless use of an automobile while under the influence of intoxicating liquor. The court ordered a new trial for defendant.

 

99 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Carleton v. Town of Framingham


M-6341


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


418 Mass. 623; 640 N.E.2d 452; 1994 Mass. LEXIS 482


May 3, 1994, Argued  
September 12, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Middlesex. Civil actions commenced in the Superior Court Department on July 6, 1988, and October 14, 1988, respectively. The cases were consolidated for trial and were tried before Richard S. Kelley, J. After review by the Appeals Court, the Supreme Judicial Court granted leave to obtain further appellate review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated action seeking recovery for the deaths of decedents killed by drunk drivers, appellant town sought review of an order of the Appeals Court (Massachusetts), which held that the public duty rule did not bar appellee beneficiaries' recovery and affirmed the jury verdicts in favor of the beneficiaries.


OVERVIEW: A town challenged jury verdicts in favor of beneficiaries in their negligence actions against the town. The question before the court was whether the amendment of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 258, §10, by Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 258, § 57, which added a statutory public duty rule providing governmental immunity, could constitutionally be retroactively applied. On appeal, the court reversed the jury verdicts. The court found that the beneficiaries' theories of liability depended upon the alleged failure of the police to prevent criminal conduct. The court also found that the state had an interest in protecting governmental entities from the expenditure of time, effort, and money in the defense of claims barred by the amendment and in protecting against recovery on such claims. The court also determined that although the beneficiaries' interests in the judgments on appeal were entitled to recognition as property rights, those interests were not as firmly established as rights of the kind that the court had protected against retroactive legislative abolition. The court further found that the beneficiaries continued to have claims against the drunk driver.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments and directed that judgments be entered in favor of the town.

 

100 of 1333 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Kwiatkowski


W-6531


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


418 Mass. 543; 637 N.E.2d 854; 1994 Mass. LEXIS 469; 29 A.L.R.5th 784


May 2, 1994, Argued  
August 3, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Worcester. Complaint received and sworn to in the Worcester Division of the District Court Department on June 1, 1992. On appeal to the jury session of that division, the case was tried before Timothy S. Hillman, J. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court (Massachusetts), where defendant challenged the stalking statute, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 265, § 43 as facially vague and hence, unconstitutional. Defendant also challenged the enforcement of a restraining order granted to his former wife.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's former wife obtained a restraining order that required the him to stay away from her residence, and to make no telephone calls to her. When he violated the terms of the order, he was arrested and subsequently found guilty of stalking, in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 265, § 43. The court agreed with defendant that the statute was unconstitutionally vague, requiring reversal of the stalking conviction. The statute announced a definition of "harasses" that could be fairly argued as requiring one to engage repeatedly in a pattern of conduct or series of acts over a period of time. Under this interpretation, one pattern or one series would not be enough. Although a single pattern of conduct or a single series of acts, combined with the other elements of the crime, was presumably intended to constitute the crime. That is not, however, stated in the statute with sufficient clarity to avoid the force of the defendant's claim of unconstitutional vagueness. None of defendant's arguments addressed his three convictions of violating a restraining order, for which he received concurrent sentences to run with the stalking conviction.


OUTCOME: The court agreed that the stalking statute was unconstitutionally vague and could not be enforced against the defendant, and his conviction of the crime of stalking must be reversed. The convictions of violating a restraining order were affirmed.

Opinion of the Justices to the House of Representatives


S-6602


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


418 Mass. 1201; 637 N.E.2d 213; 1994 Mass. LEXIS 465



July 28, 1994, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner representatives presented questions to the court to determine the constitutionality of proposed Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 55, § 6b.


OVERVIEW:  Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 55, § 6b established limits on the amount of total receipts a political candidate could raise in the years other than the year in which the candidate was running for election. The legislature had grave doubts as to the constitutionality of the bill. The court determined that the bill violated rights of free expression and association guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. I. The court found that the burden placed on the candidate's rights was not justified by a compelling state interest.


OUTCOME: The court answered the representatives questions and found that the proposed bill would have been unconstitutional.
